Subject: 
Addendum 
Additional Field Exploration 
International Market Place Redevelopment 
Waikiki, Oahu, Hawaii 
Reference: Report by Geolabs, Inc. dated February 10, 2014 
“Geotechnical Engineering Exploration 
International Market Place Redevelopment 
Waikiki, Oahu, Hawaii” 
Dear Mr. Kramer: 
This addendum present the results of our additional field exploration work 
conducted after the demolition of the International Market Place establishment in the 
Waikiki area of Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. 
This post-demolition phase of our field exploration program consisted of drilling 
and sampling two borings, designated as Boring Nos. B-213 and B-214, extending to 
depths of about 147.5 and 240 feet below the existing ground surface, respectively.  
The approximate locations of these two drilled borings are shown on the Site Plan, 
Plate 2 herein. 
Detailed descriptions of the field exploration methodology are presented in 
Appendix A of the above-referenced report prepared for the project.  Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered are presented on the Logs of Borings, 
Plates A-24 and A-25 herein.  Photographs of the core runs are presented on 
Plates E-29 through E-31 in this addendum. 
Soil and Materials Engineers, Inc. Page 2
W.O. 5232-20(Addendum)
July 2, 2014
We appreciate the opportunity to be of service to you on this project. If you have
questions or need additional information, please contact our office.
Respectfully submitted,
GEOLABS, INC. LICENSED
Teddy S.T. Kwok, P.E.
Senior Project Engineer

SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Based on our experience in the area and the subsurface boring data from our field 
explorations, the project site generally is underlain by surface fill materials overlying soft 
and/or loose lagoonal deposits extending to depths of about 50 to 60 feet below the 
existing ground surface. Below the highly compressible lagoonal deposits, our field 
explorations generally encountered a relatively thick upper coral ledge, on the order of 
about 10 to 15 feet thick, extending between depths of about 50 and 75 feet below the 
existing ground surface. It should be noted that the upper coral ledge does not appear 
continuous across the project site and likely is absent along the central portion of the 
project site, probably due to erosion by an ancient alluvial stream channel. The coral ledge 
within the channel appears to have been eroded away and has been replaced with thick 
sequences of alluvial soils extending to depths of about 150 feet (or more) below the 
existing ground surface. 
Outside the buried ancient alluvial stream channel, the lagoonal deposits and/or 
coral ledges generally were underlain by coralline detritus materials consisting of medium 
dense silty coralline sands and gravel extending to depths of over 200 feet below the 
existing ground surface. The coralline detritus materials may be interbedded with relatively 
thick dense coral formation, older alluvium and/or hard basalt rock formation in localized 
areas at depths ranging from about 95 to 180 feet below the existing ground surface. 
Groundwater was encountered at depths varying from approximately 2.0 to 7.7 feet 
below the existing ground surface at the time of our 2004 and current field explorations. 
The groundwater levels encountered generally correspond to about Elevations –0.7 to 
+4.7 feet MSL. Due to the proximity of the project site to the Pacific Ocean, water levels 
are expected to vary with tidal fluctuations. It should be noted that water levels also may 
vary with seasonal precipitation, storm surge conditions, groundwater withdrawal, and 
other factors. 
Based on the moderate to relatively high column loads (less than 100 to 3,000 kips) 
anticipated for the new structures, we believe the near-surface soils would not provide 
adequate foundation support for the proposed structures without appreciable settlements 
under the building loads. Therefore, we recommend using a deep foundation system to 
support the proposed structures planned for the IMP Redevelopment project. Based on 
the design team’s evaluation, the selected deep foundation support system will consist of 
cased micropiles. 
The micropile foundation system for support of the proposed structures will consist 
of cased micropiles with allowable compressive load capacities of 100, 200 or 300 kips per 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 5232-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page iv 
Hawaii • California 
micropile. The 100-kip and 200-kip allowable compressive load capacity micropiles will 
consist of 5.5-inch diameter cased micropiles whereas the 300-kip allowable compressive 
load capacity micropiles will consist of 7.625-inch diameter cased micropiles. The 
allowable compressive load capacity of the micropiles is for supporting dead-plus-live 
loads and may be increased by one-third (1/3) for transient loads, such as wind or seismic 
forces. In addition, a permanent casing extending to the top of the bond zone should be 
provided, and the permanent casing should have an outside diameter (OD) of about 5.5 or 
7.625 inches (same as the grout bulb size). Considering the variable subsurface 
conditions across the project site, we recommend conducting at least two static load tests 
on sacrificial micropiles during the pre-construction activities. In addition, we also 
recommend conducting proof tests on a minimum of 5 percent of the production micropiles 
to be selected by Geolabs. The load test results will be used to confirm and/or modify the 
estimated lengths of production micropiles. 
Minor structures with relatively light structural loads (requirement of less than 
40 kips per column) may be supported on shallow foundations consisting of spread and/or 
continuous strip footings. An allowable bearing pressure of up to 2,000 psf may be used 
for the design of footings bearing on the re-compacted on-site materials (minimum 
90 percent relative compaction). Footings should be embedded a minimum of 18 inches 
and a maximum of 30 inches below the lowest adjacent finished grade. Please note that 
shallow footings at the site will be susceptible to seismically induced ground settlements of 
about 5 to 10 inches. If these seismically induced ground settlements are unacceptable to 
the ownership, consideration may be given to supporting these lightly loaded structures on 
a deep foundation system similar to the other structures for the project. 
Due to the presence of loose and/or soft lagoonal deposits underlying the site at 
shallow depths, we believe the new fills will need to be placed as soon as practical to 
induce the anticipated ground settlements prior to construction of improvements and 
pavements. We anticipate only minor new fills (2 feet or less of new fills) will be required to 
raise the existing ground surface to achieve the finished grades for the proposed new 
construction. If an appreciable thickness of new fill is needed (more than 2 feet thick), a 
settlement-waiting period should be incorporated into the construction schedule for the 
development. 
The text of this report should be referred to for detailed discussions and specific 
recommendations for planning and design of the project. 
END OF SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS

SECTION 1.  GENERAL 
This report presents the results of our geotechnical engineering exploration 
performed for the International Market Place (IMP) Redevelopment project located in 
the Waikiki area of Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The general location and 
vicinity of the project site are shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
This report summarizes the findings and geotechnical recommendations derived 
from our field exploration, laboratory testing, and engineering analyses for the project. 
The recommendations presented in this report are intended for the design of 
foundations, slabs-on-grade, retaining structures, site grading, and pavements for the 
project only. In addition, discussions on the excavation and dewatering considerations 
for the project are included in this report for information purposes. The findings and 
recommendations presented herein are subject to the limitations noted at the end of this 
report. 
1.1 
Project Considerations 
The project site is at the existing world renowned International Market Place 
located in the Waikiki area of Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The proposed 
International Market Place Redevelopment project is situated within the block bounded 
by (in a clockwise direction) Kuhio Avenue, Ohana Islander Waikiki Hotel, Sheraton 
Princess Kaiulani Hotel, Kalakaua Avenue, Waikiki Beachcomber Hotel, Duke’s Lane, 
and Aqua Waikiki Wave Hotel. 
The project will consist of the design and construction of a three-level open air 
mall on the site of the current International Market Place. A three-level parking deck is 
proposed above the rear of the retail mall, creating a six-story structure with parking on 
the roof (Level 7). An anchor store is planned at the rear of the mall as well within the 
footprint of the existing Miramar Hotel. The project will consist of the demolition of the 
Miramar Hotel and the current International Market Place and construction of the 
proposed mall. The mall may be connected with an adjacent project if the adjacent hotel 
is redeveloped.

SECTION 1. GENERAL

Based on the current schematic design, significant below-grade structures are 
not planned for the project. However, we envision relatively shallow below-grade 
structures, such as elevator pits, may be required for the project. We also anticipate the 
finished floor elevation of the building structures would be set at near or slightly above 
the existing grades. Therefore, we anticipate only minor grading consisting of less than 
2 feet of fills would be required to achieve the finished grades at the project site. 
Based on the anticipated scope of work for the redevelopment project, we 
envision geotechnical recommendations would be required for the following areas: 
Provision of foundation recommendations for the new structural elements, 
such as the open air mall, parking decks, and anchor store. 
Provision of slabs-on-grade design recommendations for the new building 
construction. 
Provision of seismic design considerations for the new structures. 
Provision of earthwork recommendations for the project. 
Provision of pavement design recommendations for new driveways and 
loading docks, etc. 
Based on the anticipated subsurface conditions and the moderate to high 
structural loading anticipated for the new structures (on the order of less than 100 to 
3,000 kips per column), we recommend using a deep foundation system to support the 
proposed structures planned for the IMP Redevelopment project. Based on the design 
team’s evaluation, the selected deep foundation support system will consist of cased 
micropiles. 
In addition to the conventional geotechnical borings, three Seismic Cone 
Piezocone Penetration Test (SCPTu) probes with seismic shear wave velocity profiling 
were advanced to depths of about 97 to 187 feet as part of our field exploration 
program. The SCPTu probes were used to supplement the geotechnical borings and for 
seismic design considerations. In general, subsurface explorations consisting of SCPTu 
probes yield more accurate results for sensitive materials (such as the loose granular 
soils and soft cohesive soils at the site) because the data is obtained directly from the 
in-situ soils.

enhancements and utility line connections only. It should be noted that extensive off-site 
sewer and water system improvements are not included in the scope of work presented 
in this report. 
1.2 
Purpose and Scope 
The purpose of our explorations was to obtain an overview of the surface and 
subsurface conditions at the project site in support of the design of the International 
Market Place Redevelopment project. The subsurface information obtained was used to 
develop a generalized soil and/or rock data set to formulate geotechnical 
recommendations for the design of the proposed redevelopment project. The work was 
performed in general accordance with our Statement of Qualifications and Fee Proposal 
of September 21, 2012 and additional fee breakdown dated January 22, 2014. The 
scope of work for this exploration included the following tasks and work efforts: 
1. 
Review of available soil and geologic information for the project site and 
vicinity, including our previous field exploration conducted in 2004 at the 
project site. 
2. 
Coordination of the boring stake-out, utility toning, and clearing of the 
boring locations by a geologist from our firm. 
3. 
Coordination of our site access and field exploration work with the 
property owner’s representative, archaeologist from Cultural Surveys 
Hawaii, and representatives from dck Worldwide and Allied Builders 
System. 
4. 
Coordination with the City and County of Honolulu – Department of 
Planning and Permitting to obtain the necessary excavation and street 
usage permits for the boring location within the public right-of-way. 
5. 
Provision of police officers and safety devices for traffic control and/or lane 
closures at a boring location during our field exploration program. 
6. 
Mobilization and demobilization of truck and track-mounted drill rigs and 
operators to the project site and back. 
7. 
Drilling and sampling of twenty-three borings extending to depths of 
approximately 99 to 244 feet below the existing ground surface for a total of 
about 4,155 lineal feet of field exploration.

SECTION 2.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
2.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu is composed largely of the weathered remnants of two extinct 
shield volcanoes - Waianae and Koolau. The older volcano, Waianae, forms the bulk of 
the western third of the island while the younger shield, Koolau Volcano, forms the 
majority of the eastern two-thirds of the island. It is believed that Waianae Volcano 
became extinct while Koolau Volcano was still active, and its eastern flank is buried 
partially below Koolau lavas in Central Oahu. 
The project site is on the southern flank of Koolau Volcano, and its 
geomorphology and subsurface conditions are directly related to the glacio-eustatic 
fluctuations of the sea level during the Pleistocene Epoch and the genesis of the 
Honolulu Coastal Plain. The subsurface conditions in the Waikiki area are dominated by 
fossil Pleistocene Age coral-algal reef deposits and related deposits laid during high 
stands of the sea, which are intercalated with terrigenous sediments. The base of the 
stratigraphic section in this area is Koolau Basalt. 
During the later Pleistocene Epoch (Ice Age), there were many sea level 
changes as a result of widespread glaciation in the continental areas of the world. 
These glacio-eustatic fluctuations resulted in stands of the sea that were both higher 
and lower relative to present sea level on Oahu. About 15,000 years ago, a relatively 
rapid rise in sea level occurred. During that rise, the valleys in the project area were 
drowned. In the last 10,000 years or so, the sea level has adjusted to its present stand. 
Terrigenous and marine sediments have continued to accumulate in low-energy 
estuarine or lagoonal environments resulting in thick deposits of loose and/or soft 
sediments along the coast in the areas that were formerly valleys and drainageways. 
In the early part of this century, the Waikiki area consisted of low, marshy areas. 
As the City of Honolulu grew and the Waikiki area was urbanized, man-made fills were 
placed to reclaim the marshy areas and lagoons. The fill was placed upon lagoonal 
deposits consisting of silty sands with coral fragments. The lower clayey material was 
deposited during periodic influxes of non-marine alluvial debris, which apparently 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 5232-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 6 
Hawaii • California 
smothered the coral reef development. Land development and reclamation projects 
within the last 100 years have brought the Waikiki area to its present form. Many of the 
resulting fills are of poor quality in terms of supporting heavy structural loads. 
2.2 
Site Description 
The project site is at the existing world renowned International Market Place 
located in the Waikiki area of Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The proposed 
International Market Place redevelopment project will be situated within the block 
bounded by Kuhio Avenue, Miramar and Princess Kaiulani Hotels (on the northeast), 
Kalakaua Avenue, and the Coral Reef and Beach Comber (Beachcomber) Hotels and 
Duke’s Lane (on the southeast). 
At the time of our field exploration, the project site generally was occupied by a 
cluster of retail and commercial buildings up to two stories in height. In addition, several 
large banyan trees were observed scattered across the project site. Based on our field 
observations and the topographic survey map provided, the project site is relatively level 
with existing ground surface elevations between about +5 and +7 feet Mean Sea 
Level (MSL). 
2.3 
Subsurface Conditions 
Our office previously explored the subsurface conditions at the project site in 
2004 by drilling and sampling five borings, designated as Boring Nos. 1 through 5, 
extending to depths of about 61 to 180.5 feet below the ground surface. Our current 
field exploration program consisted of drilling and sampling twenty-three additional 
borings, designated as Boring Nos. 101 through 111 and Boring Nos. 201 through 212, 
extending to depths of about 99 to 244 feet below the existing ground/pavement 
surface. The approximate locations of the borings drilled at the project site are shown 
on the Site Plan, Plate 2. 
Based on our experience in the area and the available soil boring data from our 
2004 and current field explorations, the project site generally is underlain by surface fill 
materials consisting of medium dense silty sands extending to depths of about 8 feet 
below the existing ground surface. The surface fill materials generally were underlain by 
lagoonal deposits consisting of loose silty sands and gravel and soft sandy silts 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 5232-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 7 
Hawaii • California 
extending to depths of about 50 to 60 feet below the existing ground surface. It should 
be noted that the lagoonal deposits encountered at the project site are highly 
compressible when subjected to structural loads and are susceptible to soil liquefaction 
during a seismic event. 
Within the highly compressible lagoonal deposits, our field explorations generally 
encountered some relatively thin coral ledges (“nuisance” ledges) in localized areas of 
the project site. Based on the subsurface information obtained, these relatively thin 
“nuisance” ledges generally start at depths of about 10 to 15 feet below the existing 
ground surface and is only on the order of about 3 to 5 feet thick. 
Below the highly compressible lagoonal deposits, our field explorations generally 
encountered a relatively thick upper coral ledge, on the order of about 10 to 15 feet 
thick, extending between depths of about 50 and 75 feet below the existing ground 
surface. It should be noted that the upper coral ledge does not appear continuous 
across the project site and likely is absent along the central portion of the project site, 
probably due to erosion by an ancient alluvial stream channel. The buried ancient 
alluvial stream channel generally is oriented (longitudinally) in a northeast-southwest 
direction. The coral ledge within the channel has been eroded away and has been 
replaced with thick sequences of alluvial soils extending to depths of about 150 feet (or 
more) below the existing ground surface. The delineation of the approximate limits of 
the ancient alluvial stream channel based on our interpretation of the subsurface and 
geologic conditions is shown on the Site Plan (Plate 2). 
Outside the buried ancient alluvial stream channel, the lagoonal deposits and/or 
coral ledges generally were underlain by coralline detritus materials consisting of 
medium dense silty coralline sands and gravel extending to depths of over 200 feet 
below the existing ground surface. The coralline detritus materials may be interbedded 
with relatively thick dense coral formation, older alluvium, and/or hard basalt rock 
formation in localized areas at depths ranging from about 95 to 180 feet below the 
existing ground surface. 
For illustration purposes only, two Generalized Subsurface Profiles depicting the 
interpreted subsurface conditions at the project site are provided on Plates 3.1 and 3.2.

Groundwater was encountered at depths varying from approximately 2.0 to 
7.7 feet below the existing ground surface at the time of our 2004 and current field

Elevations –0.7 to +4.7 feet MSL. Due to the proximity of the project site to the Pacific

2.4 
Geophysical Investigations 
Surface geophysical surveys using the Multi-Channel Analysis of Surface Wave 
(MASW) and ReMi Survey methods were performed by Global Geophysicist along five 
(5) transects in an attempt to delineate the approximate limits of the buried ancient 
alluvial stream channel traversing the project site. The methodology and results of the 
geophysical investigations are presented in Appendix C. 
2.5 
Seismic Design Considerations 
The current building code adopted by the City and County of Honolulu is the 
International Building Code, 2006 Edition (IBC 2006) with amendments. Based on 
IBC 2006, the project site may be subject to seismic activity, and seismic design 
considerations will need to be addressed. 
2.5.1 Earthquakes and Seismicity 
In general, earthquakes that occur throughout the world are caused solely by shifts 
in the tectonic plates. In contrast, earthquake activity in Hawaii is linked primarily to 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 5232-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 9 
Hawaii • California 
volcanic activity. Therefore, earthquake activity in Hawaii generally occurs before or 
during volcanic eruptions. In addition, earthquakes may result from the 
underground movement of magma that comes close to the surface but does not 
erupt. The Island of Hawaii experiences thousands of earthquakes each year, but 
most of the earthquakes are so small that only sensitive instruments could detect 
the small tremors. However, some of the earthquakes are strong enough to be felt, 
and a few cause minor to moderate damage. 
In general, earthquakes (associated with volcanic activity) are most common on the 
Island of Hawaii. Earthquakes that are directly associated with the movement of 
magma are concentrated beneath the active Kilauea and Mauna Loa Volcanoes on 
the Island of Hawaii. Because the majority of the earthquakes in Hawaii (over 
90 percent of earthquakes) are related to volcanic activity, the risk of seismic 
activity and degree of ground shaking diminishes with increased distance from the 
Island of Hawaii. The Island of Hawaii has experienced numerous earthquakes 
greater than Magnitude 5 (M5+); however, earthquakes are not confined only to the 
Island of Hawaii. 
To a lesser degree, the Island of Maui has experienced numerous earthquakes 
greater than Magnitude 5. Therefore, moderate to strong earthquakes have 
occurred in the County of Maui. The effects of earthquakes occurring on the Islands 
of Hawaii and Maui may be felt on the Island of Oahu. For example, several small 
landslides occurred on the Island of Oahu as a result of the Maui Earthquake of 
1938 (M6.8). In addition, some houses on the Island of Oahu were reportedly 
damaged as a result of the Lanai Earthquake of 1871 (M7+). 
Due to the relatively short period of documented earthquake monitoring in the State 
of Hawaii, information pertaining to earthquakes that were felt on the Island of Oahu 
may not be complete. In general, over the last 150 years of recorded history, we 
are not aware of reported earthquakes greater than Magnitude 6 occurring on the 
Island of Oahu. Based on available information, we understand an earthquake of 
about Magnitude 5.6 occurred on June 28, 1948 in the vicinity of the Island of 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014

Oahu, possibly along the hypothesized and controversial Diamond Head Fault 
feature. 
The Diamond Head Fault feature is believed to extend northeasterly away from the 
southeastern tip of the Island of Oahu. The Diamond Head Fault feature may be 
related to the widely documented Molokai Fracture Zone located on the sea floor in 
the vicinity of the Hawaiian Islands. Despite only the moderate tremor intensity, the 
resulting damage was reportedly widespread and included broken windows, 
ruptured masonry building walls, and a broken underground water main. In addition, 
some areas on the Island of Oahu, including the Tantalus, Iwilei, and Tripler areas, 
reported more intense ground shaking, severe enough to have cracked reinforced 
concrete. 
2.5.2 Liquefaction Potential 
Based on the International Building Code (2006 Edition), the project site should be 
evaluated for the potential for soil liquefaction. Based on the subsurface conditions 
encountered and a design peak ground acceleration of 0.21g, liquefaction may 
occur resulting in appreciable settlements at the ground surface. Therefore, the 
effects of potential liquefaction should be taken into consideration in the design of 
the proposed redevelopment. 
Soil liquefaction is a condition where saturated cohesionless soils located near the 
ground surface undergo a substantial loss of strength due to the build-up of excess 
pore water pressures resulting from cyclic stress applications induced by 
earthquakes. In this process, when the loose saturated sand deposit is subjected to 
vibration (such as during an earthquake), the soil tends to densify and decrease in 
volume causing an increase in pore water pressure. If drainage is unable to occur 
rapidly enough to dissipate the build-up of pore water pressure, the effective stress 
(internal strength) of the soil is reduced. Under sustained vibrations, the pore water 
pressure build-up could equal the overburden pressure, essentially reducing the 
soil shear strength to zero and causing it to behave as a viscous fluid. During 
liquefaction, the soil acquires sufficient mobility to permit both horizontal and 
vertical movements, and if not confined, will result in significant deformations. 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 5232-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 11 
Hawaii • California 
Soils most susceptible to liquefaction are loose, uniformly graded, fine-grained 
sands and loose silts with little cohesion. The major factors affecting the liquefaction 
characteristics of a soil deposit are as follows: 
FACTORS 
LIQUEFACTION SUSCEPTIBILITY 
Grain Size Distribution 
Fine and uniform sands and silts are 
more susceptible to liquefaction than 
coarse or well-graded sands. 
Initial Relative Density 
Loose sands and silts are most 
susceptible to liquefaction. Liquefaction 
potential is inversely proportional to 
relative density. 
Magnitude and Duration of Vibration 
Liquefaction potential is directly 
proportional to the magnitude and 
duration of the earthquake. 
As indicated above, the site is underlain by lagoonal deposits consisting of loose 
sands and gravel with little cohesion. Based on our analysis, it appears that the 
loose granular soil deposits at the project site have factors of safety of less than 1.0 
against liquefaction based on the design earthquake. Therefore, the weak lagoonal 
deposits are susceptible to soil liquefaction during the design earthquake event. 
However, we believe lateral spreading associated with liquefaction would not be a 
significant design consideration based on the relatively flat topography across the 
site. 
2.5.3 Soil Profile 
Based on the subsurface materials encountered and our engineering analyses, the 
project site is underlain by potentially liquefiable soils. Therefore, the project site 
would need to be classified as Site Class F based on IBC 2006 
(Table No. 1613.5.2). Per Section 1613.5.5.1 of the IBC 2006, a site-specific 
response spectrum analysis will need to be performed to determine the appropriate 
values for seismic design considerations. 
2.5.4 Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
Due to the presence of loose lagoonal deposits near the ground surface, shear 
wave velocity profiling was performed in an effort to more closely analyze the

subsurface conditions for seismic design considerations. We performed shear wave 
velocity profiling using seismic piezocone penetration testing equipment (SCPTu) at 
discrete depths extending to approximately 180 feet below the existing ground 
surface at several selected locations. Based on the subsurface conditions, the 
weighted average shear wave velocity for the materials within the upper 100 feet of

2.5.5 Site-Specific Response Spectrum Analysis 
As required by IBC 2006, a site-specific elastic response spectrum (site-specific 
design ground motion) was developed using the Shake 2000 computer program for 
the one-dimensional wave propagation analysis. In general, the site-specific elastic 
response spectrum (site-specific design ground motion) was generated in general 
accordance with Chapter 21, Site-Specific Ground Motion Procedures for Seismic 
Design contained in ASCE 7-05 (Minimum Design Loads for Buildings and Other 
Structures). 
A site response model was established based on the subsurface conditions 
encountered in our borings and cone penetration test soundings at the project site. 
Because the majority of the project site (approximately three-fourths) is underlain by 
the thick sequences of alluvial soils within the buried ancient alluvial stream 
channel, an idealized soil profile representing the conditions within the ancient 
alluvial stream channel was utilized for the seismic response analyses. Non-linear 
shear stress-strain relationships were selected for each subsurface geo-material, 
as appropriate. 
Fourteen horizontal ground motion acceleration time histories were selected from 
five earthquake events with earthquake Magnitudes of 5.9, 6.0, 6.4, 6.6, and 6.7. 
Each selected time history was scaled to approximate the maximum considered 
earthquake acceleration of 0.256g expected at the project site. The ground motion 
time histories were input at the base of the soil profile and propagated through the 
soil profile to generate the site-specific ground motion time history and response 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014

Page 13 
Hawaii • California 
spectrum at the ground surface. The elastic response spectrum was calculated 
based on materials with 5% of critical damping. 
It should be noted that site-specific response spectra are dependent on the 
magnitude of the earthquake and the distance of the seismic event to the project 
site in addition to subsurface conditions. Based on our review of the geologic 
conditions, there are no known significant mapped fault zones located on the Island 
of Oahu; therefore, we do not anticipate seismic events to occur within 5 miles of 
the project site. As a result, we do not expect the site-specific elastic response 
spectrum to be affected by near-source effects from seismic events. Some of the

SECTION 3.  DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Based on our experience in the area and the subsurface boring data from our 
field explorations, the project site generally is underlain by surface fill materials 
overlying soft and/or loose lagoonal deposits extending to depths of about 50 to 60 feet 
below the existing ground surface. Below the highly compressible lagoonal deposits, our 
field explorations generally encountered a relatively thick upper coral ledge, on the 
order of about 10 to 15 feet thick, extending between depths of about 50 and 75 feet 
below the existing ground surface. It should be noted that the upper coral ledge does 
not appear continuous across the project site and likely is absent along the central 
portion of the project site, probably due to erosion by an ancient alluvial stream channel. 
The coral ledge within the channel appears to have been eroded away and has been 
replaced with thick sequences of alluvial soils extending to depths of about 150 feet (or 
more) below the existing ground surface. 
Outside the buried ancient alluvial stream channel, the lagoonal deposits and/or 
coral ledges generally were underlain by coralline detritus materials consisting of 
medium dense silty coralline sands and gravel extending to depths of over 200 feet 
below the existing ground surface. The coralline detritus materials may be interbedded 
with relatively thick dense coral formation, older alluvium, and/or hard basalt rock 
formation in localized areas at depths ranging from about 95 to 180 feet below the 
existing ground surface. 
Groundwater was encountered at depths varying from approximately 2.0 to 
7.7 feet below the existing ground surface at the time of our 2004 and current field 
explorations. The groundwater levels encountered generally correspond to about 
Elevations –0.7 to +4.7 feet MSL. Due to the proximity of the project site to the Pacific 
Ocean, water levels are expected to vary with tidal fluctuations. It should be noted that 
water levels also may vary with seasonal precipitation, storm surge conditions, 
groundwater withdrawal, and other factors. 
Based on the moderate to relatively high column loads (less than 100 to 
3,000 kips) anticipated for the new structures, we believe the near-surface soils would

not provide adequate foundation support for the proposed structures without 
appreciable settlements under the building loads. Therefore, we recommend using a 
deep foundation system to support the proposed structures planned for the IMP 
Redevelopment project. Based on the design team’s evaluation, the selected deep 
foundation support system will consist of cased micropiles. 
The micropile foundation system for support of the proposed structures will 
consist of cased micropiles with allowable compressive load capacities of 100, 200 or 
300 kips per micropile. The 100-kip and 200-kip allowable compressive load capacity 
micropiles will consist of 5.5-inch diameter cased micropiles whereas the 300-kip 
allowable compressive load capacity micropiles will consist of 7.625-inch diameter 
cased micropiles. In addition, a permanent casing extending to the top of the bond zone 
should be provided, and the permanent casing should have an outside diameter (OD) of 
about 5.5 and 7.625 inches (same as the grout bulb size). Considering the variable 
subsurface conditions across the project site, we recommend conducting at least two 
static load tests on sacrificial micropiles during the pre-construction activities. In 
addition, we also recommend conducting proof tests on a minimum of 5 percent of the 
production micropiles to be selected by Geolabs. The load test results will be used to 
confirm and/or modify the estimated lengths of production micropiles.  
Minor structures with relatively light structural loads (requirement of less than 
40 kips per column) may be supported on shallow foundations consisting of spread 
and/or continuous strip footings. An allowable bearing pressure of up to 2,000 psf may 
be used for the design of footings bearing on the re-compacted on-site materials 
(minimum 90 percent relative compaction). Footings should be embedded a minimum of 
18 inches and a maximum of 30 inches below the lowest adjacent finished grade. 
Please note that shallow footings at the site will be susceptible to seismically induced 
ground settlements of about 5 to 10 inches. If these seismically induced ground 
settlements are unacceptable to the ownership, consideration may be given to 
supporting these lightly loaded structures on a deep foundation system similar to the 
other structures for the project.

Due to the presence of loose and/or soft lagoonal deposits underlying the site at 
shallow depths, we believe that the new fills will need to be placed as soon as practical 
to induce the anticipated ground settlements prior to construction of improvements and 
pavements. We anticipate only minor new fills (2 feet or less of new fills) will be required 
to raise the existing ground surface to achieve the finished grades for the proposed new 
construction. If an appreciable thickness of new fill is needed (more than 2 feet thick), a 
settlement-waiting period should be incorporated into the construction schedule for the 
development. Detailed discussions and geotechnical recommendations for the design of 
the project are presented in the following sections of this report. 
3.1 
Micropile Foundations 
Based on the anticipated column loads and the subsurface conditions 
encountered at the project site, we believe appreciable foundation settlements would 
occur if the new structures were supported on a shallow foundation system. To reduce 
the potential for appreciable settlements and future distresses to the new structures, we 
recommend utilizing a deep foundation system consisting of cased micropiles to support 
the new structures planned for the International Market Place Redevelopment project. 
In general, the cased micropile foundation system consists of a small diameter 
(usually less than 12 inches), drilled and grouted, pile with steel reinforcing. The 
micropile foundation typically is constructed by drilling a hole (with or without casing), 
placing reinforcing steel in the hole, and grouting the hole. Micropiles are desirable 
because they can be installed readily in access restrictive environments and in 
numerous soil types and ground conditions. In addition, the micropile installation 
generally causes minimal disturbance to adjacent structures, the adjacent soils, and the 
environment. 
The micropile foundation system for support of the proposed structures will 
consist of cased micropiles with allowable compressive load capacities of 100, 200 or 
300 kips per micropile. The 100-kip and 200-kip allowable compressive load capacity 
micropiles will consist of 5.5-inch diameter cased micropiles whereas the 300-kip 
allowable compressive load capacity micropiles will consist of 7.625-inch diameter 
cased micropiles. The allowable compressive load capacity of the micropiles is for

GEOLABS, INC.

Hawaii • California 
supporting dead-plus-live loads and may be increased by one-third (1/3) for transient 
loads, such as wind or seismic forces. 
The permanent casing should have an outside diameter (OD) of at least 5.5 or 
7.625 inches (same as the grout bulb size). Due to the high load capacity of the 
micropiles, we anticipate using the high-strength (Grade 150) threaded bars for the 
micropile reinforcing steel. The following table provides a summary of the micropile

To facilitate the micropile drilling and ensure the quality of the grouting, we 
recommend advancing the steel casing to the bottom of the micropile during the drilling 
operation. The steel casing may be withdrawn during the grouting operation while a 
minimum of 5 feet grout head is maintained above the bottom of the casing at all times. 
The casing should be withdrawn above the design casing depth and plunged back to 
the design casing depth. 
3.1.1 Micropile Load Test Program 
It should be noted that the bond stress between the grout bulb and the soil is highly 
dependent on the drilling procedures and the grouting methods employed by the 
contractor to install the micropile. Therefore, the bond stress between the grout 
bulb and the soil may vary considerably between different contractors and micropile 
foundation systems. In order to determine whether the contractor’s methods of 
micropile installation are adequate and to determine the ultimate compressive load 
capacity, we recommend performing at least two pre-production compressive load 
tests on sacrificial micropiles. In general, the purpose of the pre-production load test 
on a micropile is to fulfill the following objectives: 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 5232-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 19 
Hawaii • California 
To examine the adequacy of the methods and equipment proposed 
by the contractor to install the micropiles to the depths required 
To confirm or modify the estimated minimum depth of the 
micropiles by determining the ultimate grout-to-soil bond stress 
To assess the contractor’s method of drilling and grout injection 
To evaluate the degree of long-term settlements of the micropile 
bearing in the soft to medium stiff lagoonal and alluvial soil deposits 
In general, the pre-production load tests should be performed in accordance with 
ASTM D 1143 (Quick Load Test Method). Based on experience, we believe the 
load tests should be conducted no earlier than 7 days after completion of the 
micropile installation to allow the grout adequate time to cure. Four additional 
micropiles may be used for reaction during the compressive load testing of the 
pre-production load test micropile. The reaction micropiles may be installed to 
appropriate depths to provide adequate reaction in uplift (to be determined by the 
contractor). 
The load test micropile should be loaded gradually to at least 200 percent of the 
allowable design load in compression. The micropile should be loaded in 10% 
design load increments, and each load should be held for at least 2.5 minutes. A 
creep test should be performed when the test load is at 130 percent of the design 
load and the movements have stabilized. The creep test load should be held for a 
minimum of 10 or 60 minutes. We recommend holding the maximum test load 
(200 percent of the design load) for a minimum of 60 minutes depending on the 
recorded movements of the load test micropile. The pre-production load tests are 
an integral part of the design of the micropile foundation system. Additional creep 
hold intervals may be requested based on the results of the test readings. 
Therefore, we recommend a Geolabs representative observe the pre-production 
load tests. 
In addition to the pre-production load tests, we also recommend performing pullout 
tests (proof tests) on selected micropiles during construction to confirm the load 
carrying capacity of the installed production micropiles. We recommend testing a 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 5232-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 20 
Hawaii • California 
minimum 5 percent of the total number of micropiles (approximately 36 micropiles) 
for pullout. The pullout tests should consist of subjecting the micropile to at least 
130 percent of the design load. The micropile should be loaded in 12.5% design 
load increments, and each load should be held for at least 2.5 minutes. The 
maximum test load should be held for a minimum of 10 or 60 minutes. Pullout test 
on the selected micropile is an integral part of the design of the micropile foundation 
system; therefore, we recommend conducting the pullout tests under the 
observation of a Geolabs representative. 
3.1.2 Lateral Load Resistance 
It should be noted that the micropile foundation system provides low lateral load 
resistance due to the relatively small diameter of the micropile. Therefore, the 
lateral load resistance contributed by the vertical micropiles should be calculated 
based on the micropile by the Micropile Designer of Record. Lateral loads imposed 
on the new structure foundations may be resisted primarily by passive pressure 
resistance against the vertical faces of the pile cap and grade beams and by shear 
acting along the sides of the pile caps and grade beams. 
Passive pressure resistance against the vertical faces of the pile cap and grade 
beams may be estimated using an equivalent fluid pressure of 350 pounds per 
square foot per foot of depth (pcf) above the groundwater level and 150 pcf below 
the groundwater level. The static groundwater level at the project site may be 
assumed to be at about Elevation +3 feet MSL for design purposes. Additional 
resistance to lateral loads may be provided by shear resistance between the sides 
of the pile caps and grade beams and the adjacent soils. A side shear resistance of 
up to 150 pounds per square foot (psf) may be used for transient loads such as 
wind or seismic forces only. 
3.1.3 Micropile Foundation Settlements 
Settlements of the micropiles will result primarily from elastic compression of the 
micropile member and subgrade response. We estimate the total settlement of the 
micropile-supported foundations to be about 0.75 to 1.75 inches due to the depth of 
the micropile (100 to 200 feet deep) and the high load capacity of the micropiles. 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014

Hawaii • California 
Differential settlements between micropile supported foundations should be about 
one-half of the total settlement. We believe that these settlements are essentially 
elastic and should occur as the loads are applied. 
3.1.4 Construction Considerations 
Based on the subsurface conditions at the project site, it should be noted that hard 
drilling (such as cobbles, boulders, and concrete) may be encountered locally within 
the fill materials at the site considering the urban nature of the project site and in 
the subsurface. 
A specialty subcontractor experienced in the construction of a cased micropile 
foundation system (minimum five projects) should perform installation of the 
micropiles. Due to the specialized nature of the micropile foundation construction, 
observation and testing of the micropile foundation system should be designated a 
“Special Inspection” item in accordance with Section 1704 of the IBC 2006. 
Therefore, a Geolabs representative (Special Inspector) should be present to 
observe the geotechnical aspects of the micropile foundation installation and 
testing. 
3.2 
Shallow Foundations 
We envision that minor structures with relatively light structural loads (less than 
40 kips) required for the project may be supported on shallow foundations consisting of 
spread and/or continuous strip footings. In addition, structures supported on a shallow 
foundation system should be structurally separated from the structures supported on 
deep foundations. Adjacent structures with structural connections to the commercial 
development (supported on micropiles) should be supported on the same type of 
foundation as the commercial development. 
An allowable bearing pressure of up to 2,000 pounds per square foot (psf) may 
be used for the design of footings bearing on the re-compacted on-site materials 
(minimum 90 percent relative compaction). This bearing value is for supporting dead-
plus-live loads and may be increased by one-third (1/3) for transient loads, such as those 
caused by wind or seismic forces. Footings should be embedded a minimum of 
18 inches below the lowest adjacent finished grade. In addition, the embedment of the

W.O. 5232-10

Hawaii • California 
shallow footings should be limited to a maximum of 30 inches below the lowest adjacent 
grade to reduce the potential for encountering groundwater and soft and/or loose 
lagoonal deposits at the footing subgrade level. Foundations located next to utility 
trenches or easements should be embedded below a 45-degree imaginary plane 
extending upward from the bottom edge of the utility trench, or the footings should be 
extended to a depth as deep as the inverts of the utility lines. 
If the foundations are designed and constructed as recommended herein, we 
estimate that footing settlements under the anticipated static design loads for footings 
bearing on the recompacted in-situ soils to be less than 1 inch. Differential settlements 
between adjacent footings supported on similar materials should be on the order of 
about 0.5 inches or less. Please note that shallow footings at the site will be susceptible 
to seismically induced ground settlements of about 5 to 10 inches. If these seismically 
induced ground settlements are unacceptable to the ownership, consideration may be 
given to supporting these lightly loaded structures on a deep foundation system similar 
to the other structures for the project 
Lateral loads acting on the structures may be resisted by a combination of friction 
between the base of the foundation and the bearing materials and by passive earth 
pressure developed against the near-vertical faces of the embedded portion of the 
footings. A coefficient of friction of 0.40 may be used for footings bearing on the 
re-compacted on-site fill soils. Resistance due to passive pressure may be calculated 
using an equivalent fluid pressure of 350 pcf for footings embedded in the on-site fill 
materials. This value assumes that the concrete for the footings is cast neat against 
dense on-site materials. Otherwise, the soils around the footings should be well 
compacted (minimum of 90 percent relative compaction). Unless covered by pavements 
or slabs, the passive resistance in the upper 12 inches below the finished grade should 
be neglected. 
It is important that a representative from Geolabs observe the footing 
excavations to evaluate the competency of the bearing materials and the embedment 
depths. If unsuitable materials are encountered in the footing excavations, these 
materials should be over-excavated to expose the underlying firm materials and

replaced with compacted fill. Therefore, provisions should be made in the contract

3.3 
Slabs-On-Grade 
We anticipate the floor slabs for the proposed development will consist of 
concrete slabs-on-grade. Based on the existing topography and the anticipated finished 
floor elevation, we envision the slab-on-grade for the proposed structures will be 
supported on the existing ground or shallow new compacted fills (less than 2 feet thick) 
placed to raise the existing ground surface to the finished subgrades at the building pad 
areas. 
As mentioned above, the project site could be subjected to appreciable ground 
settlements in the event of liquefaction caused by a moderate seismic event (M6+). 
Seismically induced ground settlements on the order of about 5 to 10 inches may be 
anticipated, and the building interior may sustain some damage that would disrupt the 
operation of the complex. Therefore, there exists the potential for damage and distress 
to the building slabs-on-grade under the micropile foundation support system. However, 
we believe the potential for damage may be significantly reduced by increasing the 
building slab thickness and by incorporating steel reinforcement into the concrete slab. 
As discussed above, concrete slabs-on-grade may be subjected to ground 
settlements and distress in the event of seismically induced soil liquefaction. As an 
alternative to slabs-on-grade, consideration may be given to structurally supporting the 
ground floor slab on the micropile foundations to reduce the potential for damage to the 
interior of the building in the event of seismically induced ground settlements. 
New fills needed to raise the site to the design grades should be placed in 
general accordance with the recommendations provided in the “Site Grading” section of 
this report. New fills needed to raise the site to the finished subgrades will induce 
ground settlements as a result of consolidation of the underlying soft and/or loose 
lagoonal deposits. Therefore, we recommend that the new fills be placed as soon as 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 5232-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 24 
Hawaii • California 
practical to allow the anticipated ground settlements to occur prior to building and 
slab-on-grade construction. 
For interior building slabs (not subjected to vehicular traffic), we recommend 
providing a minimum 4-inch thick layer of cushion fill consisting of open-graded gravel 
(ASTM C 33, No. 67 gradation) below the slabs. The open-graded gravel cushion fill 
would serve as a capillary moisture break and would provide uniform support of the 
slabs. To reduce the potential for future moisture infiltration through the slab and 
subsequent damage to floor coverings, an impervious moisture barrier is recommended 
on top of the open-graded gravel cushion layer. It is also recommended that interior 
walls be designed to incorporate some flexibility to accommodate a small amount of 
possible ground movements. 
Where the slabs will be subjected to vehicular traffic, such as the loading dock or 
other similar areas, we recommend that a 6-inch layer of aggregate subbase be 
provided below the slabs in-lieu of the 4-inch thick gravel cushion layer. The aggregate 
subbase should consist of crushed basaltic aggregates compacted to a minimum of 
95 percent relative compaction. For design of structural slabs supported on 6 inches of 
aggregate subbase, a modulus of subgrade reaction of about 200 pounds per square 
inch per inch of deflection (pci) may be used for the compacted aggregate subbase. 
Where slabs are intended to function as rigid pavements for trucks, a minimum slab 
thickness of 6 inches may be used for preliminary design purposes. Provisions should 
be made for proper load transfer across the slab joints that will be subjected to vehicular 
traffic. 
The thickened edges of slabs adjacent to unpaved areas should be embedded at 
least 12 inches below the lowest adjacent grade. It should be emphasized that the 
areas adjacent to the slabs should be backfilled tightly against the edges of the slabs 
with relatively impervious soils. These areas should also be graded to divert water away 
from the slabs and to reduce the potential for water ponding around the slabs. 
3.4 
Retaining Structures 
Based on the information provided, we understand retaining structures, such as 
elevator and escalator pits, will be required for the proposed new development. 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS

Page 25 
Hawaii • California 
Parameters for design of foundations for retaining structures that are structurally 
connected to (or abutting) the building structures supported on micropile foundations 
should be designed in accordance with the “Micropile Foundations” section herein. 
Design of foundations for the other walls (not structurally connected to or abutting the 
building structures) may be based on the parameters presented in the following 
“Retaining Structure Foundations” subsection. 
3.4.1 Retaining Structure Foundations 
Based on the medium dense surface fill soils encountered at the site, we believe 
shallow continuous strip footings may be used for support of low retaining walls 
planned for the project. An allowable bearing pressure of up to 2,000 psf may be 
used for the design of shallow foundations bearing on the recompacted surface 
fill soils. This bearing value is for dead-plus-live loads and may be increased by 
one-third (1/3) for transient loads, such as those caused by wind or seismic 
forces. 
Wall footings should have a minimum width of 18 inches. In addition, wall 
footings on relatively flat areas should be embedded a minimum depth of 
24 inches below the lowest adjacent finished grade. In addition, the embedment 
of the shallow footings should be limited to a maximum of 30 inches below the 
lowest adjacent grade. The bottom of the footing excavations should be 
recompacted to a minimum of 90 percent relative compaction to provide a 
relatively firm and smooth bearing surface prior to the placement of reinforcing 
steel or concrete. 
If soft and/or loose materials are encountered at the bottom of the footing 
excavations, the soft and/or loose materials should be over-excavated to expose 
the underlying firm and/or dense materials. The over-excavation may be 
backfilled with on-site soils compacted to a minimum of 90 percent relative 
compaction, or the footing bottom may be extended down to bear on the 
underlying competent materials. 
Lateral loads acting on the retaining structure may be resisted by friction 
developed between the bottom of the foundation and the bearing soil and by 
IMP Geotech Report - 02/10/2014
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 5232-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 26 
Hawaii • California 
passive earth pressure acting against the near-vertical faces of the foundation 
system. A coefficient of friction of 0.40 may be used for footings bearing on the 
compacted granular fill soils. Resistance due to passive earth pressure may be 
estimated using an equivalent fluid pressure of 350 and 150 pcf for above and 
below groundwater conditions, respectively. This assumes that the soils around 
the footings are well compacted. Unless covered by pavements or slabs, the 
passive resistance in the upper 12 inches of soil should be neglected. 
3.4.2 Static Lateral Earth Pressures 
Retaining structures, such as the elevator and escalator pits and/or other retaining 
walls, should be designed to resist the lateral earth pressures due to the adjacent 
soils and surcharge effects. The recommended lateral earth pressures for design of 
retaining structures, expressed in equivalent fluid pressures of pounds per square 
foot per foot of depth (pcf), are presented below. The static groundwater level at the 
site may be assumed to be at about Elevation +3 feet MSL for design purposes.
